<p>The steppe eagle is sometimes regarded as solitary but is frequently seen in the company of conspecifics throughout the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Besides the obvious breeding pair, they often <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">flock</a> during migration and <a href="/wiki/Aggregation_(ethology)" title="Aggregation (ethology)">aggregate</a> in occasionally ample numbers during non-breeding times, usually at fruitful feeding sites, sometimes briefly cooperating with one another especially to <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">klepoparasitize</a> other birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Steppe eagles fly with slow, deep and stiff-looking wing beats, holding wings fully extend on upstrokes, rendering a heavier flight pattern than <a href="/wiki/Spotted_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Spotted eagle">spotted eagles</a>. The flight of the steppe eagle is well-analyzed such as experiments with a captive male and observations of migrants in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carruthers_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carruthers-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gillies_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillies-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> It appears that the underwing coverts operate as a high-lift device and probably provide stability through unsteady maneuvers, otherwise positive loading on the wings can be maintained. Whilst soaring, generally the wings are held flattish or slightly flexed but sometimes with the hands lowered.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carruthers_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carruthers-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gillies_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillies-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> About 90% of flight by these eagles in Israel was gliding or soaring.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> They often fly with the head dropped with hands arched in a <a href="/wiki/Gliding_flight" title="Gliding flight">glide</a> or often arms straight out and hands drooped.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gillies_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillies-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The drooping wing flight method, peculiar to the steppe eagle as well as to the <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted eagle</a>, is sometimes also called the "tuck", and is thought to be a gust response precipitated by a transient drop in aerodynamic loading.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Steppes adapt their flight to wind and thermal conditions as was studied in Israel, increasing their gliding airspeed under strong thermal convections or opposing winds. This study determined that a combination of circling in thermals and inter-thermal gliding was interspersed with soaring in straight-line glide. Israeli migrants flew up to 1,600&#160;m (5,200&#160;ft) above the ground but 90% were under 1,000&#160;m (3,300&#160;ft) and half were below 400&#160;m (1,300&#160;ft). The Israeli steppe eagles were able to maintain a mean climbing rate of 1.9&#160;m (6.2&#160;ft) per second, a mean cross-country air speed of 12.4&#160;m (41&#160;ft) per second and a mean of 15.6&#160;m (51&#160;ft) per second in glides; the flight was similar as in other common raptors here but the steppe eagle attained the highest mean cross-country speeds.<sup id="cite_ref-Spaar_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spaar-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Steppe eagles tend not to be very vocal especially when not breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Their main call is a raspy bark which is similar to that a tawny eagle, despite being mildly deeper. In aerial displays, a loud whistle has been recorded, quite unlike any vocalization of a tawny eagle. Other call recorded have included mainly low and croaking notes aside from a high shriek when startled.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_steppe_eagle_(Aquila_nipalensis)_-63.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-63.jpg/220px-The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-63.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-63.jpg/330px-The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-63.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-63.jpg/440px-The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-63.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4248" data-file-height="3392" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_steppe_eagle_(Aquila_nipalensis)_-63.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steppe eagle photographed in Nepal, arguably their primary point of passage in the eastern part of the range.</div></div></div>
<p>Steppe eagles appeared to have evolved the strategy of <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrating</a> from their breeding grounds, due in large part to the temporary seasonal availability of their main prey, <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Louchart_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louchart-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> They probably migrate in greater numbers than any other eagle in the world and can appear to be frequent enough at migration sites that they may mask less numerous migrating eagles that are mistakenly missed in their ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The migratory behaviour of this species is arguably amongst the best-studied aspect of its entire biology.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Welch_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welch-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Autumn migration often begins around October on fairly broad <a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">fronts</a>, and may peak around late October.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It usually ends in late November to December but steppe eagles frequently travel somewhat <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadically</a> while not breeding and so individuals may not reach their winter terminus point until about January.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Spring migration usually commences in February, peaking early from late February to March, with likely all gone from Africa by the end of the latter month, then continuing in a diminishing trickle into April and May.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In passage at Suez, the steppe eagle is one of the earlier migrating raptors on average alongside the <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo rufinus</i>), averaging about a month sooner in passage than the <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>) (the most common migrant there) and slightly sooner than the lesser spotted eagle, as well as much sooner than some other raptors there.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> On average, the wintering period in Africa is relatively brief, at a mean of about up to about 4 months (down to about 2), while adult steppe eagles spend up to 7 months (max of around 5 months for a young eagle) on their breeding grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GPS_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GPS-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In autumn records from Africa, younger eagles migrate the earliest and adults the latest.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Javed_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Javed-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Radio-tagging studies confirmed, much as in the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a>, that in spring juveniles migrated later, wandering about more so and came back to the summering grounds much later.<sup id="cite_ref-Meyburg_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyburg-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> One young steppe eagle that was banded in passage in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> wintered initially in Yemen before returning for the summer to Kazakhstan, then migrating to eastern Africa the following winter, showing that they can change their migratory habits over time.<sup id="cite_ref-Javed_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Javed-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Many studies corroborate that steppe eagles generally migrate lesser distances as they age.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brooke_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brooke-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peak movements around the Red Sea show as many as 76,000 steppe eagles moving over Bab-el-Mandeb in the fall of 1987, with up to 65,000 (in 1981) in Suez and up to 75,000 in <a href="/wiki/Eilat" title="Eilat">Eilat</a>, Israel in the year 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Once migrating steppe eagles enter Africa in autumn, no mass migrations have been recorded anywhere for the species in the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Although not large, some semi-significant spring movements were detected in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, despite none being recorded in the fall.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In autumn, steppe eagles usually pass over Bab-el-Mandeb in the north of Red Sea while in spring they predominantly cross to the south of the Red Sea around Suez.<sup id="cite_ref-Meyburg_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyburg-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The mean number of steppe eagles that annually pass over Eilat in spring are estimated at 28,032 with a mean peak day of 10 March, making them roughly the fourth most common migrating raptor in spring there (and they often pass in intermingled flocks with other soaring raptors, but not those with powered flight).<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In Eilat, steppe eagles constitute 6.4% of all raptors seen, nearly all of the <i>Aquila</i> eagles seen and, among those that could be aged, an estimated 60–70% of the steppes seen were thought to be adults.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> More unusually, the steppe eagle may be the only raptor to also use Israel as a common migratory flight path in autumn as well as spring, with even commoner migrating raptors such as <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">European honey buzzards</a> (<i>Pernis apivorus</i>) being rare there in the fall.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In Nepal over 2.5 weeks starting in 20 October, nearly 7852 steppe eagles were tallied, making up more than 80% of the recorded migrating raptors, with peak times of movement being between 10:00 AM and 4:00 PM, especially between noon and 2:00 PM.<sup id="cite_ref-DeRoder_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeRoder-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Over 3 years of study in Nepal, 21,447 steppe eagles were recorded (as many as 1102 within a day and a mean of about 15.2 an hour) at the counting sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Subedi_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Subedi-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Strong evidence of east-to-west migratory movements, rather than south or northbound, has been made in the <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu_Valley" title="Kathmandu Valley">Kathmandu Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> It was indicated based on the directional studies that especially juveniles from the eastern part of the breeding may be more frequently migrate westbound to reach wintering areas such as the Middle East and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Subedi_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Subedi-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, juveniles and subadults during the wintering season seem to considerably outnumber adults in the Indian subcontinent so many do head due south.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Of 3381 ageable steppe eagles in passage in Nepal, 56% were juveniles or immature, 44% were adults; of 7852 eagles, 58% migrated in groups of 1–5, 30% in 5–20 groups and 12% in larger flocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DeRoder_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeRoder-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a> of India, about 11,000 steppe eagles were recorded in autumn migration in 2001 and about 40% less were counted the next spring. This study indicated different migratory paths being used in the seasons, presumably following the winds predominant direction around the terrain, with the westerly autumn migration mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Himalayas" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Himalayas">western Himalayas</a> and the easterly spring migration more so in the east of Nepal.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Staging areas are not well-delineated in India but appear to concentrate around feeding sites such as landfills.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande1_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande1-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> A single female that was radio-tagged in Mongolia was recorded to travel southwest and stop in southeastern Tibet, which is also the southernmost part of the species breeding range. The data from this female indicated that not all steppe eagles move to warmer climates and, based on that she remained stationary until her return to Mongolia, that she was not nomadic as many eagles of the species are.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> During return spring migration, the steppe eagles in passage in Nepal will reportedly amass into groups of approximately 5 to 20 eagles at only about 20 to 70&#160;m (66 to 230&#160;ft) above the terrain before rising up to cross between the snow-covered peaks.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_steppe_eagle_(Aquila_nipalensis)_-_l66.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-_l66.jpg/220px-The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-_l66.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-_l66.jpg/330px-The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-_l66.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-_l66.jpg/440px-The_steppe_eagle_%28Aquila_nipalensis%29_-_l66.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5400" data-file-height="3600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_steppe_eagle_(Aquila_nipalensis)_-_l66.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An unusual, pale probable subadult steppe eagle in Nepal.</div></div></div>
<p>16 radio-tagged eagles that returned in their first spring migration to their Kazakh summering grounds were recorded to winter as first-year juveniles either, in roughly equal measure, in the Arabian Peninsula or southern Africa, and covered straight-line distances, ranging from 3,489 to 9,738&#160;km (2,168 to 6,051&#160;mi), although individually could meander up to 20,644&#160;km (12,828&#160;mi) for one eagle migrating from wintering grounds Botswana. Of the 16 returning Kazakhstan eagles, spring migration lasted an average of 40 days, ranging individually from 38 to 54 days and covered a mean of 355&#160;km (221&#160;mi) each day. The migration path generally led the eagles around almost every direction of the Red Sea, many also passing over Israel and some wrapping around the Caspian Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Meyburg_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyburg-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> A different radio-tagging study of 19 juveniles (about 57% of which survived) from Russian or Kazakh sites found that autumn movements in the 1st year migration averaged 4,222&#160;km (2,623&#160;mi) and confirmed not only that they freely changed wintering sites anywhere from India to southern Africa but they never returned, surviving or not, to their natal site in the 1st year, instead return to wandering widely across the northern steppe. The 1st migration averaged 52 days and were much briefer for females than for males, with the discrepancies more pronounced for eagles originating from the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a>. 15 birds tracked in this study were found to have migrated most frequently to winter in south Pakistan (right along the borderlands to India) or in eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>. Spring migration began on a mean date of 25 March for the 15 young eagles and lasted about 26 days on average, covering a mean of 3,925&#160;km (2,439&#160;mi), with females initiating migration on average 18 days later than males and migrating more briefly, more quickly and more often with fewer stops than males. 9 eagles which were tracked successfully in their first spring passage in this study wandering widely mostly in natural steppe hunting for squirrels and 8 of these tracked to their 2nd autumn migration took about 1.5 times shorter on their 2nd autumn passage and migrated about 17% less far on average.<sup id="cite_ref-GPS_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GPS-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spermophilus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Spermophilus.jpg/220px-Spermophilus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Spermophilus.jpg/330px-Spermophilus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Spermophilus.jpg/440px-Spermophilus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2808" data-file-height="1861" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spermophilus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ground squirrels like <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_ground_squirrel" title="Long-tailed ground squirrel">long-tailed ground squirrels</a> are often the most important prey for breeding steppe eagles.</div></div></div>
<p>The steppe eagle is an opportunistic predator like other <i>Aquila</i> eagles but has a number of dietary and foraging peculiarities. They prey mainly on small-sized mammals, with some <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and (mostly in winter) frequently <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Despite their opportunistic nature, the steppe eagle is a somewhat specialized predator on particular mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a> while breeding and, during non-breeding times, feeds on various foods but is often peculiarly narrow in dietary selection, preferring massed food sources that require little effort for them to obtain.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Various other small or medium-sized mammals can be become the most significant prey locally on the breeding grounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pika" title="Pika">pikas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zokor" title="Zokor">zokors</a> and, generally more secondarily, <a href="/wiki/Marmot" title="Marmot">marmots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerbil" title="Gerbil">gerbils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> During the breeding season, one resource claimed that prey mostly weighs 50 to 250&#160;g (1.8 to 8.8&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Another account estimated that about 95% of prey weighed less than 250&#160;g (8.8&#160;oz), although predominantly over 63&#160;g (2.2&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> However, yet another resource claimed that staple prey for steppe eagles could weigh anywhere from 50&#160;g (1.8&#160;oz) up to 1,500&#160;g (3.3&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Even the latter estimate may be conservative in size range, with prey species varying widely in size from very small insects from colonies to unexpectedly large mammals (and seldom birds) apparently killed near nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, a preference has indeed been detected for smaller <a href="/wiki/Burrow" title="Burrow">burrowing mammals</a> (i.e. probably under 250&#160;g (8.8&#160;oz) or so). Studies have determined where only larger species of burrowing mammals are predominant (even the larger species of ground squirrel), the steppe eagles appear to attain comparatively sparse nest densities, only occurring in high densities where the smaller burrowers are profuse.<sup id="cite_ref-Account_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Account-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">Ecological partitioning</a> to limit interspecific competition may be a factor that dictates the steppe eagle's preference for relatively small prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The breeding steppe eagle mainly hunts in a low soaring or gliding flight, at a maximum of 200&#160;m (660&#160;ft), diving or making short, accelerated stoops onto their prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Usually, they tend to capture their prey on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Steppe eagles have been recorded in both Kazakhstan and Mongolia to tactfully avoid casting a shadow before descending onto prey and may drop stones to provide a distraction, a probable form of <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">tool use</a>. In the Kazakh observation, the steppe eagles quickly became used to agricultural activity adjacent to prey accesses while they hunted.<sup id="cite_ref-Ivanov_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanov-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tingay_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tingay-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> They also may hunt in any season on the ground, moving with a shambling gait as necessary, and may give chase on foot to both vertebrate and insect prey. Steppe eagles can often ambush prey by standing in wait next to <a href="/wiki/Burrow" title="Burrow">burrows</a>, suddenly pouncing quickly onto the quarry upon its emergence.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Steppe eagles have been seen in China to buzz through <a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locust</a> swarms on the wing as well as to taking avian prey from over 200&#160;m (660&#160;ft) above the ground in a dive.<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">Tandem hunting</a> by pairs has been recorded during the breeding season while, in winter and migration, these may be the most social of all eagles, often sharing by up to the dozens abundant food sources. The non-breeding steppe eagle flocks may even seem to assist one another in procuring prey from which they themselves are not likely to be able to directly profit and may repeatedly assist each other until all flock members are satiated. If confirmed, this mutually beneficial foraging strategy between presumably unrelated eagles is truly unique.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tingay_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tingay-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Much like the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_eagle" title="Tawny eagle">tawny eagle</a>, the steppe eagle will readily rob other raptors of their catches, approaching from any angle and pursuing closely until the victim is forced to land or drop its food.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Summer_Diet">Summer Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Summer Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The single prey species most strongly associated with the steppe eagle is the <a href="/wiki/Little_ground_squirrel" title="Little ground squirrel">little ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus pygmaeus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In some areas, as much as 98% of the diet reportedly can be little ground squirrels.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> This is a smallish ground squirrel though it is actually not greatly smaller than many other Eurasian ground squirrels, at a mean adult weight of about 235.2&#160;g (8.30&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Hayssen_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayssen-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The little ground squirrel once reached densities of around 30–40 per <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a> and provided a reliable food source for these eagles. However, this species has plummeted in population density, in <a href="/wiki/Kalmykia" title="Kalmykia">Kalmykia</a> for instance going down from abundant in diverse habitats to perhaps <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">locally extinct</a> before gradually trickling back up in numbers (which continue to be a mere shadow of what they once were).<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The local steppe eagles of Kalmykia continue to show a strong preference for little ground squirrels.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> A continued primary reliance on little ground squirrels by steppe eagles was also found recent in studies from <a href="/wiki/Saratov_Oblast" title="Saratov Oblast">Saratov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baskunchak" title="Lake Baskunchak">Lake Baskunchak</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabachishin_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabachishin-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich2_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich2-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Out of Russian, in the <a href="/wiki/Karaganda_Region" title="Karaganda Region">Karaganda Region</a> of Kazakhstan, little ground squirrels again were an important identified food source, at 19.25% of 400 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In the general area between the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, 112 prey items were led by little ground squirrels, at just over 33%. However, in this data, the little ground squirrels were closely followed in number (29.7%) by the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_ground_squirrel" title="Yellow ground squirrel">yellow ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus fulvus</i>), which, with seasonal weights ranging from 500 to 2,000&#160;g (1.1 to 4.4&#160;lb), is the largest of Eurasian ground squirrels.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hayssen_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayssen-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> The little ground squirrel is only found in a substantial portion of the western part of the range, so elsewhere steppe eagles tend to prey on different prey species while breeding, though generally continue to take small burrowing mammals, of course.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG/220px-Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG/330px-Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG/440px-Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2733" data-file-height="1761" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Daurischer_Pfeifhase_Ochotona_dauurica.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Small mammals like <a href="/wiki/Daurian_pika" title="Daurian pika">Daurian pika</a> are often taken in significant numbers by steppe eagles.</div></div></div>
<p>Around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Balkhash" title="Lake Balkhash">Lake Balkhash</a> in Kazakhstan, the main prey was reportedly the <a href="/wiki/Red-cheeked_ground_squirrel" title="Red-cheeked ground squirrel">red-cheeked ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus erythrogenys</i>), a slightly larger ground squirrel than the little species at a mean adult weight of 355&#160;g (12.5&#160;oz). Other prey noted here included <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_pika" title="Pallas&#39;s pika">Pallas's pika</a> (<i>Ochotona pallasi</i>), <a href="/wiki/Libyan_jird" title="Libyan jird">Libyan jird</a> (<i>Meriones libycus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Tolai_hare" title="Tolai hare">tolai hare</a> (<i>Lepus tolai</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Hayssen_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayssen-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovich_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovich-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, reportedly the main prey species is the 415&#160;g (14.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_ground_squirrel" title="Long-tailed ground squirrel">long-tailed ground squirrel</a> (<i>Urocitellus undulatus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hayssen_142-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayssen-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai</a> region, the leading prey may be the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_zokor" title="Siberian zokor">Siberian zokor</a> (<i>Myospalax myospalax</i>), which is the size of a large ground squirrel at an adult weight of about 453&#160;g (0.999&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin4_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin4-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> However, some report in the Altai region that the main prey is the long-tailed ground squirrel and the migration arrival times do seem to correspond closely with this species hibernation emergence period.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitrofanov_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitrofanov-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Another potential primary prey resource is reportedly the <a href="/wiki/Gray_marmot" title="Gray marmot">gray marmot</a> (<i>Marmota baibacina</i>). All the primary prey in the previously little reported Altai population are as adults well over what is considered the typical prey size range for this eagle, such as long-tailed ground squirrels, zokors and marmots as well as <a href="/wiki/Willow_ptarmigan" title="Willow ptarmigan">ptarmigan</a>, and in turn, this may favor the large size of the steppe eagles from this region.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mitrofanov_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitrofanov-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barashkova_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barashkova-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, other predominant prey in steppe eagle nests can be even smaller than ground squirrels. In Mongolia, the main prey by a large margin was reportedly the <a href="/wiki/Brandt%27s_vole" title="Brandt&#39;s vole">Brandt's vole</a> (<i>Lasiopodomys brandtii</i>), which weigh about 40&#160;g (1.4&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Transbaikal" title="Transbaikal">Transbaikal</a> region, the main prey may be the <a href="/wiki/Daurian_pika" title="Daurian pika">Daurian pika</a> (<i>Ochotona dauurica</i>), which weighs about 155&#160;g (5.5&#160;oz). This pika can account for around 39%, as was the case in 62 prey items, (and perhaps up to 62% locally) of the diet in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Another study reported a very different primary food source for the Transbaikal, which was the young of the much larger <a href="/wiki/Tarbagan_marmot" title="Tarbagan marmot">Tarbagan marmot</a> (<i>Marmota sibirica</i>), which were estimated in the study to be from 55 to 77% of the annual diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Peshkov_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peshkov-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Even more conflicting data found that some Transbaikal steppe eagles derived as much as 70% of their foods from long-tailed and <a href="/wiki/Daurian_ground_squirrel" title="Daurian ground squirrel">Daurian ground squirrels</a> (<i>Spermophilus dauricus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> It is possible that in both Altai and Transbaikal that the shifts to differing reported primary prey species are responses of the eagles to shifting prey availabilities as many burrowing mammals are subject to <a href="/wiki/Population_cycle" title="Population cycle">population cycles</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human-sourced depletions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> While rodents and some lagomorphs are usually favored in the diet, in some areas steppe eagles can live at least in part off of quite different prey such as <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_hedgehog" title="Long-eared hedgehog">long-eared hedgehogs</a> (<i>Hemiechinus auritus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Other notable prey taken regularly whilst breeding by steppe eagles includes <a href="/wiki/Steppe_pika" title="Steppe pika">steppe pika</a> (<i>Ochotona pusilla</i>) (especially in the Volga region), <a href="/wiki/Alpine_pika" title="Alpine pika">alpine pika</a> (<i>Ochotona alpina</i>), <a href="/wiki/Yellow_steppe_lemming" title="Yellow steppe lemming">yellow steppe lemming</a> (<i>Eolagurus luteus</i>) (especially in eastern Kazakhstan), or the slightly larger types of gerbil such as <a href="/wiki/Great_gerbil" title="Great gerbil">great gerbils</a> (<i>Rhombomys opimus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_gerbil" title="Mongolian gerbil">Mongolian gerbils</a> (<i>Meriones unguiculatus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Account_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Account-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The study of the Karaganda region of Kazakhstan with 400 prey items found illustrated that the steppe eagle is capable of deriving a living from a wide range of prey, with the foods led by <a href="/wiki/Rosy_starling" title="Rosy starling">rosy starling</a> (<i>Pastor roseus</i>) (mostly fledglings), at 24%, unidentified <i><a href="/wiki/Microtus" title="Microtus">Microtus</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>, at 19.75%, followed by little ground squirrels, unspecified pikas (8.25%), <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hares</a> (<i>Lepus europaeus</i>) (5%) and <a href="/wiki/Grey_partridge" title="Grey partridge">grey partridges</a> (<i>Perdix perdix</i>) (4.5%).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> An aptitude for avian prey was detected in Transbaikal particularly, including <a href="/wiki/Daurian_partridge" title="Daurian partridge">Daurian partridge</a> (<i>Perdix dauurica</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_quail" title="Japanese quail">Japanese quail</a> (<i>Coturnix japonica</i>) (the latter at up to 15.6% of the diet).<sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In Altai, assorted <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> (at up to 24.2% of the diet), probably mostly <a href="/wiki/Rook_(bird)" title="Rook (bird)">rooks</a> (<i>Corvus frugilegus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpie</a> (<i>Pica pica</i>), were important to diet as were <a href="/wiki/Willow_ptarmigan" title="Willow ptarmigan">willow ptarmigan</a> (<i>Lagopus lagopus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mitrofanov_152-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitrofanov-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Within the Saratov area, medium-sized birds were frequently reported in the diet, such as grey partridges, <a href="/wiki/Little_bustard" title="Little bustard">little bustards</a> (<i>Tetrax tetrax</i>), <a href="/wiki/Northern_lapwing" title="Northern lapwing">northern lapwings</a> (<i>Vanellus vanellus</i>) and rooks.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabachishin_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabachishin-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> A diversity of small passerines has been found in the diet, especially fledgling-age <a href="/wiki/Lark" title="Lark">larks</a> of various species, most frequently perhaps in Kazakhstan and Mongolia.<sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> A few reptiles found in the diet around nest have included at least <a href="/wiki/Sand_lizard" title="Sand lizard">sand lizard</a> (<i>Lacerta agilis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Caspian_whipsnake" title="Caspian whipsnake">Caspian whipsnake</a> (<i>Dolichophis caspius</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Vipera_ursinii" title="Vipera ursinii">steppe viper</a> (<i>Vipera ursinii</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marmota_baibacina.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Marmota_baibacina.jpg/220px-Marmota_baibacina.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Marmota_baibacina.jpg/330px-Marmota_baibacina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Marmota_baibacina.jpg/440px-Marmota_baibacina.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marmota_baibacina.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Larger prey such as <a href="/wiki/Gray_marmot" title="Gray marmot">gray marmots</a> are infrequently targeted by steppe eagles.</div></div></div>
<p>On occasion, during summer, a steppe eagle may be able to take exceptionally large prey. The most regular large prey to appear in their diets are usually Tolai hare, at about 2&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb), and assorted <a href="/wiki/Marmot" title="Marmot">marmots</a>. The upper size of marmots that the steppe eagle may attack is not well-established although all of the species regularly hunted by these eagles have extremely large average adult weights, i.e. around 8&#160;kg (18&#160;lb), but the typical size taken by this eagle is more likely around 1.5&#160;kg (3.3&#160;lb) (i.e. for small emergent juvenile marmots).<sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovich_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovich-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peshkov_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peshkov-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The steppe eagle takes a diversity of mammalian <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivores</a> including <a href="/wiki/Mountain_weasel" title="Mountain weasel">mountain weasel</a> (<i>Mustela atlaica</i>), <a href="/wiki/Marbled_polecat" title="Marbled polecat">marbled polecat</a> (<i>Vormela peregusna</i>), <a href="/wiki/Steppe_polecat" title="Steppe polecat">steppe polecat</a> (<i>Mustela eversmanii</i>), kits of <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_badger" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian badger">Eurasian badger</a> (<i>Meles meles</i>) and possibly live adults (i.e. "fresh remains") of 2.7&#160;kg (6.0&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Corsac_fox" title="Corsac fox">corsac fox</a> (<i>Vulpes corsac</i>) and 6&#160;kg (13&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red fox</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> A surprisingly range of young <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a> have also been found in small numbers and it is likely that some are taken both as carrion and as kills, including <a href="/wiki/Goitered_gazelle" title="Goitered gazelle">goitered gazelle</a> (<i>Gazella subgutturosa</i>), <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_gazelle" title="Mongolian gazelle">Mongolian gazelle</a> (<i>Procapra gutturosa</i>), <a href="/wiki/Saiga_antelope" title="Saiga antelope">saiga antelope</a> (<i>Saiga tatarica</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">domestic goat</a> (<i>Capra aegagrus hircus</i>). In newborns of these species, weights can vary from around 2&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb) (in goats) to about 3.5&#160;kg (7.7&#160;lb) (in saiga antelope).<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The taking of large birds is less well-documented than predation on large mammals and in some cases both in summer and during non-breeding times certainly pertain to nestling predations, such as on <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">storks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crane_(bird)" title="Crane (bird)">cranes</a>, or to pilfering easy large <a href="/wiki/Fowl" title="Fowl">fowl</a> like <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chickens</a> (<i>Gallus gallus domesticus</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Domestic_turkey" title="Domestic turkey">domestic turkeys</a> (<i>Meleagris gallopavo</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-breeding_diet">Non-breeding diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Non-breeding diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hodotermes_mossambicus,_alaat_in_water.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Hodotermes_mossambicus%2C_alaat_in_water.jpg/220px-Hodotermes_mossambicus%2C_alaat_in_water.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Hodotermes_mossambicus%2C_alaat_in_water.jpg/330px-Hodotermes_mossambicus%2C_alaat_in_water.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Hodotermes_mossambicus%2C_alaat_in_water.jpg/440px-Hodotermes_mossambicus%2C_alaat_in_water.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3468" data-file-height="2150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hodotermes_mossambicus,_alaat_in_water.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Hodotermes" title="Hodotermes">harvester termite</a>, the most significant wintering food for southern African steppe eagles.</div></div></div>
<p>The steppe eagle, despite being one of the most numerous and widely distributed of all eagles, is exceptionally poorly studied in its non-breeding dietary habits. This is due in large part to the nomadic behaviour displayed by most (but not all) steppe eagles during these times. Steppe eagles are fairly different from related species, being rather gregarious and non-predatory while away from their breeding grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Exceptionally, some steppe eagles have been known to overwinter in <a href="/wiki/Altai_Town" title="Altai Town">Altai Town</a>, Kazakhstan, living reportedly off of <a href="/wiki/Brown_rat" title="Brown rat">brown rats</a> (<i>Rattus norvegicus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Rock_dove" title="Rock dove">rock doves</a> (<i>Columba livia</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> They are often seen congregating at feeding sites with easily obtained foods that are available in large quantities. In <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>, these eagles are often associated with <a href="/wiki/Surface_weather_analysis" title="Surface weather analysis">rain fronts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a> that accompanies them.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> They do this largely to exploit a certain food source, <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termite</a> <a href="/wiki/Alate" title="Alate">alates</a>. Termites are known to emerge more extensively in these conditions and so the steppe eagle, not unlike other long-distance migrant raptors, can become locally rather <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivorous</a> to the exception of virtually any other foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dowsett_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dowsett-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jensen_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Most often, these eagles will fly down when it is noticed that termites are emerging or wait on foot and then grab them.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jensen_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> According to one account these large eagles feed on termites "lumbering after their minuscule quarry in ludicrous fashion".<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> They have also sometimes been seen to take termites in the air and feed on them in flight, not any easy task for such a large eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Roosts near termite colonies can contain several steppe eagles which may remain over days but generally depart whether well-fed or not if the rains disperse. In <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, the roosts used were the tops of quite small trees of only 2 to 3&#160;m (6.6 to 9.8&#160;ft) height.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jensen_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Although tiny with an average estimated weight of only 0.15&#160;g (0.0053&#160;oz), the <a href="/wiki/Hodotermes" title="Hodotermes">harvester termite</a> (<i>Hodotermes mossambicus</i>) (the main termite prey) have been deemed highly nutritious with a relatively high caloric value.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> It has been estimated that a steppe eagle would have to eat approximately 1600–2200 termites a day, which can be attainable in about 3 hours of feeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The stomachs of 2 dissected steppe eagles contained 630 and 930 termite heads, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In Zimbabwe, steppe eagles have also been seen in feeding masses in stubble fields picking out insects.<sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> However, it would reductive to consider the steppe eagle largely insectivorous in winter, since disproportionately the eagles seen feeding on termites in southern Africa were juveniles and immatures and many of the species winter outside of southern Africa; often wintering steppe eagles from other areas do not seem to live predominantly on insects.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">east Africa</a>, the diet of steppe eagles is poorly documented but is reported to consist largely of <a href="/wiki/Silvery_mole-rat" title="Silvery mole-rat">silvery mole-rats</a> (<i>Heliophobius argenteocinereus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Blesmol" title="Blesmol">blesmols</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Cryptomys" title="Cryptomys">Cryptomys</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-BOW_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOW-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meheretu2019_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meheretu2019-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Routine predation, probably on young or weak individuals, by steppe eagles has been recorded amongst <a href="/wiki/Flamingo" title="Flamingo">flamingo</a> colonies in east Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In several parts of Africa, steppe eagles may routinely visit and feed off of the colonies of the super-abundant bird, the <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_quelea" title="Red-billed quelea">red-billed quelea</a> (<i>Quelea quelea</i>), with a noted focus on picking off the seemingly innumerous nestlings and fledglings of this small passerine. The steppe eagles will reportedly do so by ungracefully scrambling amongst the branches of the nesting colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BOW_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOW-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Indian subcontinent, the steppe eagle appears to fulfill the role of a weakly predatory opportunist.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Individual Indian wintering steppe eagles are reported to feed at times of vulnerability of prey, including injured birds, eggs and young <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> from heronries, while groups of the eagles often occur around <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>, masses of stranded <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poultry_farming" title="Poultry farming">poultry farms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">garbage dumps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> carcass dumps.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Chari-Dhand_Wetland_Conservation_Reserve" title="Chari-Dhand Wetland Conservation Reserve">Chari-Dhand wetlands</a>, as many as 1000 steppe eagles have been seen to gather, presumably living largely off of vulnerable water birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> At the city dumps of <a href="/wiki/Pune" title="Pune">Pune</a> as many as 200 steppe eagles have been known to gather and feed.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande1_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande1-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> A carcass dump in Jorbeer near <a href="/wiki/Bikaner" title="Bikaner">Bikaner</a> was recorded to host an average of 43 steppe eagles per day during winter, with a peak number generally occurring in January and February (common dates from November to March and more rarely from September to May), with as many as 136 steppe eagles plus at least 9 other large raptors (mostly vultures), many of which are considered threatened species. It was found the Jorbeer carcass dumps enticed the steppe eagles to venture away from the normal wetland or wetland-adjacent areas used by steppe eagles in the area to the desert-like region, but <a href="/wiki/Free-ranging_dog" title="Free-ranging dog">feral dogs</a> could, in some years, appear to chase off and cause the eagles to avoid this dump.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sharma_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> A concentration of around 50 steppe eagle was seen to feed on swarms of <a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locusts</a> in Nepal.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps to avoid competition (i.e. from <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">vultures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal">jackals</a> and so on) and to monopolize a food item, steppe eagles in India appear to come largely to smaller carcasses such as those of <a href="/wiki/Jungle_cat" title="Jungle cat">jungle cats</a> (<i>Felis chaus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Python_(genus)" title="Python (genus)">pythons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Banni_Grasslands_Reserve" title="Banni Grasslands Reserve">Banni Grasslands Reserve</a>, steppe eagles are reported to largely hunt for food unlike in many other Indian reports, mainly on <a href="/wiki/Lesser_bandicoot_rat" title="Lesser bandicoot rat">lesser bandicoot rats</a> (<i>Bandicota bengalensis</i>), although also sometimes stole prey from other raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, active predation was unusually reported in <a href="/wiki/Saurashtra_(region)" title="Saurashtra (region)">Saurashtra</a> and on larger prey including <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">mongoose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_hare" title="Indian hare">Indian hare</a> (<i>Lepus nigricollis</i>) as well as an unsuccessful attack on a <a href="/wiki/Mountain_gazelle" title="Mountain gazelle">mountain gazelle</a> (<i>Gazella gazella</i>) fawn.<sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg/220px-Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg/330px-Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg/440px-Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Eagle_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steppe eagle appearing to scavenge amongst a garbage dump; among all <i>Aquila</i> eagles, it appears to have the least discerning diet and least predatory demeanor.</div></div></div>
<p>In the region of <a href="/wiki/Bharatpur,_Rajasthan" title="Bharatpur, Rajasthan">Bharatpur, Rajasthan</a>, largely around <a href="/wiki/Keoladeo_National_Park" title="Keoladeo National Park">Keoladeo National Park</a>, the foraging activities of steppe eagles have been observed extensively. The steppe eagles seldom actively hunted, instead alternating between capturing nestlings from the <a href="/wiki/Heronry" title="Heronry">heronries</a>, especially nearly fledgling-age young of late nesting <a href="/wiki/Painted_stork" title="Painted stork">painted storks</a> (<i>Mycteria leucocephala</i>), and engaging in <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a> towards other birds of prey, often doing so in groups of about three to nine eagles. More infrequently, steppe eagles in Bharatpur have been seen hunting <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">flocking</a> birds, fish (usually stranded), <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>. The steppes have been observed feeding on freshly killed young water birds at Bharatpur at daybreak and during early mornings and so may hunt while taking <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">advantage</a> of bright moonlight. Piracy against other raptors often resulted in food wastage, since the steppe eagles often forced the other raptors to drop their catch but the steppes were unable to intercept them and the kills were frequently lost into the water. In Bharatpur, the steppe eagles tended to perch relatively low compared to other eagles, at about 9 to 10&#160;m (30 to 33&#160;ft) in the trees, and to perch often for longer periods than other raptors, apparently while watching closely the activity of the other birds of prey. Of a total of 49 observed hours of activity for steppe eagles in Bharatpur, 45% of it was spent foraging, with a maximum foraging time of 69% during January, then reduced in March to only 17%. The daily food intake of individual steppe eagles was extremely low relative to their size, at only 141&#160;g (5.0&#160;oz). Instead of piracy, the steppe eagles often engaged each other in what can be considered a play display, almost exclusively between juvenile steppe eagles. In it, two birds circled 100&#160;m (330&#160;ft) or more, the higher bird circling closer and dropping toward the lower bird with extended feet, forcing it to roll over and present talons, they either immediately disengage with or without locking talons or descend looked for a few metres before separating; often steppes will fly purposely at a conspecific that is circling and fly up to a higher position so it can drop onto the other; in another incident, a steppe grabbed a plastic bag and let it go buffeting by the wind, then repeatedly caught it and let it go again, ultimately being joined by 5–6 other steppes in the "game'.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji2_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji2-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Less study has been conducted on feeding habits of the wintering and migrating steppe eagles in the <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>. What is known suggests that they, even more strongly than wintering steppe eagles in Indian subcontinent, today frequent various waste food sources inadvertently provided to them by humans. In <a href="/wiki/Muscat" title="Muscat">Muscat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, migrants largely from Kazakhstan were recorded to live off a mixture of refuse from the region's main landfill and large-scale carcass dump sites. As in the carcass dump areas of the Indian subcontinent, these carcass dumps often host a wide array of large birds of prey, both migrating species and non-migratory ones. In keeping with its size, steppe eagles dominated slightly smaller eagles and vultures and were in turn dominated by slightly larger <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eagles</a> and much larger <a href="/wiki/Lappet-faced_vulture" title="Lappet-faced vulture">vultures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Prohl_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prohl-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> High use of <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">slaughterhouses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> dump sites was recorded in winter in Iran. Interestingly, the Iranian slaughterhouses and dump sites hosted no first-year juveniles and few adults, but many steppe eagles either aged to 2 to 3 years of age (62.5%) or 4 to 5 years of age (33.3%).<sup id="cite_ref-Shafaipour_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shafaipour-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Foraging in both dump sites and available wetlands has been recorded in Iraq as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Al-Sheikhly_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Al-Sheikhly-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Incidental feeding observations from <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> suggests that steppe eagles in passage and in winter there are able to capture large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> or pirate them or similar small prey from smaller species of birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Adamian_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adamian-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecific_predatory_relationships">Interspecific predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Interspecific predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg/220px-20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg/330px-20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg/440px-20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5222" data-file-height="3472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:20191213_Ptaki_padlino%C5%BCerne_w_rezerwacie_Jor_Beed_po_Bikanerem_0936_8329.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three steppe eagles seen perched near an Indian carcass dump with another large scavenging bird, the <a href="/wiki/Griffon_vulture" title="Griffon vulture">griffon vulture</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The steppe eagle shares its distribution with several other birds of prey that can compete for resources. Most similar in feeding niche are largely other <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, many of which are also similarly migratory. One eagle of similar central distribution is the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The imperial eagle has a similar morphology and can broadly overlap in food selection.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It also takes many ground squirrels but is generally less specialized on them during breeding, and often takes similar or larger numbers of prey such as <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a> and assorted birds both large and medium. In general, the dietary biology is better understood, prey is taken of more diverse sizes and the prey spectrum is far more diverse (perhaps nearly three times as many recorded prey species) in the imperial species.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Imperial_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imperial-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The average weight taken of prey like young marmots is similar in both eagles, averaging 1.5&#160;kg (3.3&#160;lb) in the eastern imperial while the steppe also takes marmots of around this size.<sup id="cite_ref-Imperial2_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imperial2-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Although not common, the imperial eagle can sometimes take prey weighing over 2&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb), probably rather more frequently than the steppe eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Imperial2_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imperial2-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> It is possible that the steppe eagle gained the preference for relatively more numerous and social but quite small mammals as prey to avoid heavier competition over slightly larger but often more dispersed terrestrial mammals (i.e. hares, hedgehogs, etc.), especially those taken by imperial eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Account_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Account-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Also, the imperial eagle is rather more predatory in food obtainment while wintering, not infrequently eschewing the more vulnerable nestling water birds in the Indian subcontinent to take many adult birds such as <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_coot" title="Eurasian coot">coots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The eastern imperial eagle differs most significantly from steppe eagles in nesting habits, favoring tall trees, sometimes in fairly well-wooded areas, which quite contrary to the steppe eagles ground nesting preferences.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The migratory course used by imperial eagles is largely the same as the steppe eagle but the imperial is the far less numerous migrant (also more frequently overwintering near their breeding ground) and radiates less far in winter (especially in Africa).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Despite the steppe eagle averaging scarcely smaller, data from both breeding and wintering areas indicates that the imperial eagle tends to be behaviorally dominant over steppe eagles. This has manifested in full or partial displacement of steppe eagles locally using <a href="/wiki/Transmission_tower" title="Transmission tower">pylons</a> as nesting sites by imperial eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, at shared feeding sites, the steppe eagle tends to back down to the imperial eagle, often allowing it to feed first despite occasional displacement of imperials with full crops.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prohl_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prohl-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shafaipour_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shafaipour-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> On occasion, in India, steppe eagles succeed in pirating prey from imperial eagles, normally in cooperating parties of steppe eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> In at least one case in India, the steppe eagle was the aggressor in an interaction with an eastern imperial eagle, causing the two eagles to lock talons and cartwheel down with uncertain results.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> While the eagles are expected to correspond their sizes in hierarchy when nests are located in the same general area, with the steppe considered as generally subordinate to the imperial which is itself subordinate to the golden eagle, interactions in the Altai region suggest a more complex interspecific relationship. There one study reported several aggressive interactions with both imperial and golden eagles and the steppes were surprisingly the aggressors in each. In one instance, a golden eagle was fiercely attacked by a steppe eagle who appeared to dominate the interaction, grabbing the more formidable-armed golden in air and driving it forcefully to the ground (although the golden eagle was not killed).<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much has been written about what separates the steppe from the tawny eagle but little to no interactions between the species have been noted in the wild.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Beyond being strongly <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric</a>, wintering steppe eagles usually use slightly different habitats, favoring available wetlands quite apart from the arid wooded savanna and semi-desert areas preferred by
