tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle, despite being smaller and proportionately similar in talon size (with a considerably less massive gape), is a rather more powerful and bold predator than the steppe eagle, alternating between capturing relatively large prey, pirating prey from other raptors and scavenging on carrion.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The prey sizes taken by tawny eagles are perhaps the most evenly distributed across all weight classes besides the eastern imperial and golden eagles amongst <i>Aquila</i> and <i>Clanga</i> species, with a focus on prey weighing from 0.5 to 4&#160;kg (1.1 to 8.8&#160;lb), i.e. well over the typical prey sizes for steppe eagles in any area.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles occasionally attend the same food sources as wintering steppes, such as carcass dumps, other carrion and termite alates, and appear to largely ignore each other; on the other hand, an assertive steppe can sometimes displace a tawny eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Apart from imperial eagles, steppe eagles were said to be dominant over other <i>Aquila</i> eagles and spotted eagles in the guild of raptors in Bharatpur.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> The steppe eagle is quite similar to the lesser and greater spotted eagles in migratory behaviour, but tends to specialize on entirely different prey during breeding. The spotted eagles tend to nest in well-wooded areas near water and catch diverse prey, although usually focus on fairly small prey as does the steppe eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The mean prey sizes taken by greater spotted eagles, with a diet often focused on various water-friendly <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> and medium-sized birds, is probably quite similar to that of the steppe eagles, whilst that of lesser spotteds, focused on <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> and small <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, is expectedly smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Especially in Africa, lesser spotted eagles become locally specialized termite eaters very much like the steppe eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_115-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Spotted eagles appear to be almost invariably dominated by steppe eagle, as has been recorded at carcass dumps during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Prohl_178-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prohl-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> In Bharatpur, spotted eagles of all three species are quite often the targets of piracy by steppe eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_175-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_vulture" title="Egyptian vulture">Egyptian vultures</a> (<i>Neophron percnopterus</i>) appear to be subordinate to steppe eagles at carrion but most other vultures are larger (sometimes considerably so) and may be avoided by steppe eagles, although they often fed at carcass dumps alongside assorted vultures.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharma_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prohl_178-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prohl-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Many other diurnal raptors may share the ground squirrel and other prey that the steppe eagle often subsists on but are generally less specialized and tend to use different nesting habits, usually nesting in trees. These may include <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcons</a> (<i>Falco cherrug</i>), <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzards</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Buteo" title="Buteo">buzzards</a> while larger <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a> and smaller <a href="/wiki/Upland_buzzard" title="Upland buzzard">upland buzzards</a> (<i>Buteo hemilasius</i>) often nests in rocks at considerably more elevated altitudes (although the golden may too nest in trees and other habitats).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg/220px-Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg/330px-Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg/440px-Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4905" data-file-height="3629" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Family_by_Mashin_Rostislav.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">Red fox</a> make their home in the steppe habitat quite often (as seen) and both the steppe eagle and fox are known to threaten each other, especially the young of the other predator.</div></div></div>
<p>Smaller raptors like <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harriers</a> are often the only other diurnal birds of prey to regularly nest on the ground and may co-occur over much of the range of steppe eagles, although usually use damper parts of the steppe as nesting habitats than the eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Zapata_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Zapata-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Harriers also often use similar migration routes as do the steppe eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> In Africa, steppe eagles are often found feeding peaceably in the midst of the numerous <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_kite" title="Yellow-billed kite">yellow-billed kite</a> (<i>Milvus aegyptius</i>) on termites.<sup id="cite_ref-Jensen_166-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> However, when interactions are of a more competitive nature, the steppe eagle tends to dominate any species of <a href="/wiki/Milvinae" title="Milvinae">kite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Other raptors both large and small are not infrequently the victims of <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a> by steppe eagles. In India, <a href="/wiki/Brahminy_kite" title="Brahminy kite">Brahminy kites</a> (<i>Haliastur indus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kites</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>), <a href="/wiki/Laggar_falcon" title="Laggar falcon">laggar falcons</a> (<i>Falco juggar</i>), <a href="/wiki/Montagu%27s_harrier" title="Montagu&#39;s harrier">Montagu's harrier</a> (<i>Circus pygargus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harriers</a> (<i>Circus aeruginosus</i>) among others were robbed of their catches as well as spotted and even <a href="/wiki/Tawny_eagle" title="Tawny eagle">tawny eagles</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/House_crow" title="House crow">house crows</a> (<i>Corvus splendens</i>) often robbed the steppe eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In Armenia, <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montagu%27s_harrier" title="Montagu&#39;s harrier">Montagu's harriers</a> were seen to be robbed of catches by steppe eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Adamian_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adamian-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Even the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> has seen to have its prey be stolen by steppe eagles in the <a href="/wiki/Bale_Mountains" title="Bale Mountains">Bale Mountains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Predatory interactions with other carnivorous animals where the steppe eagles are victims are largely restricted to the vulnerable young, with the nest sites often being highly vulnerable due to their often accessible positions on mildly elevated ground. Like other <i>Aquila</i> eagles, when the nest is breached, parent steppe eagles may defend their nest but more often briefly flee on the wing.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The steppe eagle parent, due presumably to the vulnerability of its nest site, may take longer to return to their nest than other related eagles, further exacerbating the likelihood of the young being lost.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Hungry canids are often particularly detrimental predators, particularly <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">grey wolves</a> (<i>Canis lupus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a> (often <a href="/wiki/Herding_dog" title="Herding dog">herding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free-ranging_dog" title="Free-ranging dog">feral</a> ones) and more infrequently other <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a> and other carnivores like <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">kin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Zapata_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Zapata-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zhumageldinovich_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhumageldinovich-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin6_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin6-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> An unexpected source of steppe eagle nestling mortality was found to be from the unusually aggressive <a href="/wiki/Pallid_harrier" title="Pallid harrier">pallid harrier</a> (<i>Circus macrourus</i>) which attacked and killed two consecutive young eagles although never fed on them (possibly due to delayed displacement by the parent eagles).<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Apart from the vulnerable young on the nesting ground, steppe eagles appear to be seldom killed by natural predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> However, one was reported as the victim of a <a href="/wiki/Caracal" title="Caracal">caracal</a> (<i>Caracal caracal</i>) in Saudi Arabia (probably in a nighttime ambush).<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> More often, the steppe eagle is the predator rather than victim in deadly contests against other predators. Besides the many aforementioned accounts of prey including carnivores like mustelids and foxes, steppe eagles can also on occasion kill other raptorial birds and seems to consider even quite formidable species as viable prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Karaganda" title="Karaganda">Karaganda</a> region alone, the local steppe eagles were recorded to prey on <a href="/wiki/Lesser_kestrel" title="Lesser kestrel">lesser kestrels</a> (<i>Falco naumanni</i>), <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owls</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>) and seven <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In the Altai region, in addition to eagle-owls, the black kite has also been recorded as steppe eagle prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In fact, the steppe eagle apparently is the only bird to have preyed upon Eurasian eagle-owls besides the golden eagle on multiple occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Although rarely observed to halt movements or to eat while migrating in Israel, one steppe eagle was seen to suddenly strike down and consume an adult <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> while both species were in passage there.<sup id="cite_ref-Weiss2_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss2-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Brahminy_kite" title="Brahminy kite">Brahminy kite</a> that seen attempting to <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mob</a> a steppe eagle in <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> was observed to be killed by the eagle, while at least one other Brahminy there was also injured by an aggressive steppe eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis;_Baikonur_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Aquila_nipalensis%3B_Baikonur_01.jpg/220px-Aquila_nipalensis%3B_Baikonur_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Aquila_nipalensis%3B_Baikonur_01.jpg/330px-Aquila_nipalensis%3B_Baikonur_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Aquila_nipalensis%3B_Baikonur_01.jpg/440px-Aquila_nipalensis%3B_Baikonur_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2782" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis;_Baikonur_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult steppe eagle on its nest in <a href="/wiki/Baikonur" title="Baikonur">Baikonur</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The steppe eagle, like most raptors, breeds in pairs. Otherwise, it displays a preference for solitude whilst summering on the steppe.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Like other <i>Aquila</i> eagles, this species may engage in a territorial aerial display. The display of the steppe eagle is not well-known but can be assumed to resemble that of sympatric eagles and is known to include high circling (but perhaps engage in less aerial acrobatics than other <i>Aquila</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In Kalmykia, the mean number of pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) was 1.7.<sup id="cite_ref-Kalmykia_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kalmykia-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The steppe eagle is rare in the <a href="/wiki/Saratov_Oblast" title="Saratov Oblast">Saratov area</a>, with peak areas such as <a href="/wiki/Alexandrovo-Gaysky_District" title="Alexandrovo-Gaysky District">Alexandrovo-Gaysky District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novouzensky_District" title="Novouzensky District">Novouzensky District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ozinsky_District" title="Ozinsky District">Ozinsky District</a> hold a mean of about 3 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) while elsewhere in Saratov, the mean number of pairs per that area is about 0.8.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabachishin_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabachishin-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Nearest neighbor distance in Transbaikal averaged 6.61&#160;km (4.11&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> 85 nests in the Altai foothills were found to distanced at a mean of 2,040&#160;m (6,690&#160;ft) although not all of the nests were occupied.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Ukok_Plateau" title="Ukok Plateau">Ukok Plateau</a> (within the Altais), the mean nearest neighbor distance was found to be 3.15&#160;km (1.96&#160;mi), ranging from 1.09 to 8.06&#160;km (0.68 to 5.01&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov2_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov2-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Another study of the Altai found that there were about 0.51 to 3.11 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) with a number of successful pairs of 0.35–1.35 per this area, and further more found that <a href="/wiki/Khakassia" title="Khakassia">Khakassia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Krasnoyarsk_Krai" title="Krasnoyarsk Krai">Krasnoyarsk Krai</a> contained higher nesting densities but that the <a href="/wiki/Tuva" title="Tuva">Tvya Republic</a> contained lowered densities. This study found that in Altai that the mean nearest neighbor distance was on average 4.91&#160;km (3.05&#160;mi), ranging from 1.91 to 17.4&#160;km (1.19 to 10.81&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin5_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In the borderlands between Kazakhstan and Russia, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Aktobe_Region" title="Aktobe Region">Aktobe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orenburg" title="Orenburg">Orenburg</a>, there was an estimated 7.1 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin6_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin6-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> In the southern part of the Aktobe region, such as between <a href="/wiki/Bayganin_District" title="Bayganin District">Bayganin District</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miyaly" title="Miyaly">Miyaly</a>, the density of nesting steppes can occasionally reach as high as 2-2.5 pairs per 1&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (0.39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Boyko_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyko-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Atyrau_Region" title="Atyrau Region">Atyrau Region</a> of Kazakhstan, nests on utility towers were spaced at a mean of around 5.3&#160;km (3.3&#160;mi) as opposed to around 10 to 15&#160;km (6.2 to 9.3&#160;mi) apart on other nesting substrates.<sup id="cite_ref-Gissov_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gissov-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> In the Aral and Caspian areas of Kazakhstan, the mean nearest neighbor distance was 6.31&#160;km (3.92&#160;mi) but the density of breeding pairs varied more than 50 fold based on habitat, with locally cliff habitat being the least productive and clay semi-desert the most productive.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Within the <a href="/wiki/Karaganda_Region" title="Karaganda Region">Karaganda Region</a>, the mean number of pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) was 7.67 while the numbers of successful pairs per such an area was 3.24.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, home ranges were found to range in size from 4.5 to 54&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1.7 to 20.8&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The breeding season falls from late March or early April (occasionally not starting in earnest until late April) to roughly late August, although several steppe eagles can remain on their breeding grounds until at least October.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mitrofanov_152-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitrofanov-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tabachishin_145-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabachishin-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nests">Nests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg/220px-%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg/330px-%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg/440px-%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D0%90_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E15_-_%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B4_%D1%81_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%93%D0%BD.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elevated portions of land, whether rocky or not, are the typical nesting site for steppe eagles.</div></div></div>
<p>The nest is a large stick platform, varying greater in size based on available materials but averaging flatter than those of other <i>Aquila</i> eagles, excepting the tawny eagle. Most nests are around 70 to 100&#160;cm (28 to 39&#160;in) in diameter and around 20 to 50&#160;cm (7.9 to 19.7&#160;in) deep.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Nests in the <a href="/wiki/Transbaikal" title="Transbaikal">Transbaikal</a> averaged 118&#160;cm (46&#160;in) across with nests located on cliffs or rocks getting larger at up to 50 to 85&#160;cm (20 to 33&#160;in) deep.<sup id="cite_ref-Peshkov_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peshkov-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> In the Sarastov area, 14 nests measured on average 92&#160;cm (36&#160;in) in diameter by 27&#160;cm (11&#160;in) in depth, with ranges of 80 to 125&#160;cm (31 to 49&#160;in) and 15 to 35&#160;cm (5.9 to 13.8&#160;in), respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabachishin_145-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabachishin-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Nests in Xinjiang could react diameters of as much as 180&#160;cm (71&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The largest nest in the southern Aktobe region reached a diameter of 2.4&#160;m (7.9&#160;ft); while height of the nest could vary there from 10 to 90&#160;cm (3.9 to 35.4&#160;in) those in trees could reach up to 80 to 106&#160;cm (31 to 42&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Boyko_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyko-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> The nest is generally lined with twigs and much clutter. This is due to sparser nesting material in their habitat, so nest structures frequently include peculiar items: <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">polyethylene</a> bags, pieces of <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a>, old <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">rags</a> and other human <a href="/wiki/Garbage" title="Garbage">refuse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The nest is traditionally place in an exposed site among <a href="/wiki/Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone">stones</a>, often on a <a href="/wiki/Hummock" title="Hummock">hummock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Other nesting sites can include very low <a href="/wiki/Shrub" title="Shrub">bushes</a> and a spot on the ground which is usually raised slightly above the mean layout of the environment. Some other nest sites are known have including <a href="/wiki/Hay" title="Hay">haystacks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ruin" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruin">ruins</a> for a slight prominence, also sometimes on a non-steep <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">cliff</a> or rarely in a <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">tree</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Although some older studies claimed that steppe eagles avoided nesting near human activity this has been largely disproven. In Kalmykia, all nests were only 50 to 100&#160;m (160 to 330&#160;ft) from paved roads.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> A nest in the <a href="/wiki/West_Kazakhstan_Region" title="West Kazakhstan Region">West Kazakhstan Region</a> was found to be quite close to a village.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> However, in Transbaikal, the conversion of areas to farmland was a primary cause of a large population contraction for the eagles there.<sup id="cite_ref-Peshkov_159-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peshkov-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Of 14 nests in Kalmykia, 10 were located on the ground and 4 were in trees or shrubs, not to mention some located on transmission towers at a mean nest height of 11.7&#160;m (38&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In Transbaikal, 53.7% of 47 nests were on hills.<sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Those located on rocky outcrops in Transbaikal, averaged 1.95&#160;m (6.4&#160;ft) above the mean ground level.<sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In the region of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baskunchak" title="Lake Baskunchak">Lake Baskunchak</a>, most of 16 nests found for the species were on rocky rubble and boulders, <a href="/wiki/Karst" title="Karst">karst</a> <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_crater" title="Volcanic crater">craters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">cliffs</a>, with two located on trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich2_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich2-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In the Altai region, 62.4% of territories contained only one nest but 27.4% contained 1 alternate nests, 4.7% contained 2 alternate nests and 5.9% contained 3 alternate nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> A study in Altai determined that nests were often in virgin or fallow steppe near <a href="/wiki/Larch" title="Larch">larch</a> forests, and were often reused in subsequent years. The location of Altai nests were on gently sloping rocky <a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">outcrops</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Cuesta" title="Cuesta">cuesta</a> <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">escarpments</a>, mostly, which were about 82% of known nest sites (with only 4% on flat ground)<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mitrofanov_152-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitrofanov-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin5_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In the borderlands of Kazakhstan and Russia, of 418 total nests, 75.6% of nests were on <a href="/wiki/Ridge" title="Ridge">ledges</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boulder" title="Boulder">boulders</a> or <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a> ridges and only 15.8% were on flat ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin6_199-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin6-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Within the region of <a href="/wiki/Atyrau_Region" title="Atyrau Region">Atyrau</a> in Kazakhstan, 26% of raptor nests located on electrical <a href="/wiki/Transmission_tower" title="Transmission tower">transmission towers</a> (or pylons) were those of steppe eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Gissov_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gissov-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> 38% of nests in the Aral-Caspian region were either on pylons or at the base of them.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the Karaganda Region, 75.6% of nests were on <a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">outcrops</a> and a further 10.84% were on rock disintegrations, and all nest sites had a rocky substrate (including a very small amount on low <a href="/wiki/Shrub" title="Shrub">bushes</a> as well). The Karaganda nests were at a mean elevation of 528.7&#160;m (1,735&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In the general <a href="/wiki/West_Kazakhstan_Region" title="West Kazakhstan Region">West Kazakhstan Region</a>, of 286 nests 30.42% were on ground or rocks, 28.32% in trees or bushes and27.27% on utility poles.<sup id="cite_ref-Coexistence_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coexistence-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The nests in the West Kazakh area that were on rocks and cliffs averaged 10.55&#160;m (34.6&#160;ft) above the ground, those on poles averaged 13&#160;m (43&#160;ft) above ground, in trees and those in bushes averaged 2.09&#160;m (6.9&#160;ft) above the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Coexistence_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coexistence-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> One nest near the <a href="/wiki/Ili_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Ili River">Ili River</a> was noted to be a small tree, <i><a href="/wiki/Haloxylon" title="Haloxylon">Haloxylon</a></i>, while another was a perhaps dangerously hot sandy <a href="/wiki/Dune" title="Dune">dune</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> One successful nest near the <a href="/wiki/Irtysh_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Irtysh River">Irtysh River</a> was on the shrub <a href="/wiki/Spiraea" title="Spiraea">Iberian meadowsweet</a> (<i>Spiraea hypericfolia</i>) (while another unsuccessful one was on a thick growth of <i><a href="/wiki/Lonicera_tatarica" title="Lonicera tatarica">Lonicera tatarica</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich3_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich3-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Another unusual Kazakh nest was on the ground with rather <a href="/wiki/Loam" title="Loam">loamy</a> <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a> that was probably unsafe due to excessive sun exposure (only blocked for 20% of the day) and a considerable local presence of red foxes.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Of 49 nests found in Mongolia 47.8% were on the ground, 32.6% were on rock columns or large boulders, 8.7% on cliffs, 8.7% on artificial substrates, including a car <a href="/wiki/Tire" title="Tire">tire</a>, an abandoned car cabin and an artificial nest platform and 2.2% was in a tree. All Mongolian nest in this study were at elevations between 1,100 and 2,500&#160;m (3,600 and 8,200&#160;ft), with a mean of 1,415&#160;m (4,642&#160;ft). In Mongolia, the height of nests above the surrounding flat earth was 2.28&#160;m (7.5&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs_and_incubation">Eggs and incubation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Eggs and incubation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis_egg,_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Aquila_nipalensis_egg%2C_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg/220px-Aquila_nipalensis_egg%2C_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Aquila_nipalensis_egg%2C_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg/330px-Aquila_nipalensis_egg%2C_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Aquila_nipalensis_egg%2C_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg/440px-Aquila_nipalensis_egg%2C_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3201" data-file-height="2244" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis_egg,_Collection_Museum_Wiesbaden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg, Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<p>The clutch size is usually 2, i.e. from 1 to 3 with some clutches very rarely including as many as 4–5 eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The clutch averaged 2.38 in nests in the Aral-Caspian area.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In Kalmykia, the mean clutch size was 2.31.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In the Lake Baskunchak area, 66.7% of nests contained 2 eggs, 25% contained 3 and 8.3% contained 1.<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich2_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich2-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Out of 30 inhabited nests in the Transbaikal, 77% had 2 eggs, 20% had 1 egg and 3% had 3 eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Peshkov_159-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peshkov-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> In the Volga area, the average clutch size was 2.2.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabachishin_145-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabachishin-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> One study in Altai found the clutch size of a small sample averaged 1.67, while another placed the mean clutch size of 19 clutches was 2.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mitrofanov_152-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitrofanov-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Yet another Altai study found that clutch size in 32 active nests was 2.33.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin5_207-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In Aktobe and Orenburg, the mean clutch size was 1.94.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin6_199-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin6-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> In western Kazakhstan, the clutch size average was 2.38, with up to 4 recorded; here 54.05% of clutches contained 2 eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Coexistence_211-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coexistence-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The mean clutch size in study in Mongolia was 1.9, amongst 43 egg-laying pairs, 58.1% laid 2 eggs, 23.3% laid 1 and 18.6% laid 3.<sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are largely off-white in colour but may have faint brown or grey spots.<sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The mean egg size in Kalmykia was 66.52&#160;mm ×&#160;53&#160;mm (2.619&#160;in ×&#160;2.087&#160;in) with a range of 61.4 to 73.5&#160;mm (2.42 to 2.89&#160;in) in height and a range of 51.0 to 57.2&#160;mm (2.01 to 2.25&#160;in) in width.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The average was similar in the Volga area, at 67.5&#160;mm ×&#160;54.6&#160;mm (2.66&#160;in ×&#160;2.15&#160;in), with ranges of 63.1 to 72.5&#160;mm (2.48 to 2.85&#160;in) by 52.1 by 55&#160;mm (2.05 by 2.17&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Tabachishin_145-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabachishin-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Eggs were larger in Transbaikal where they measured on average 72.5&#160;mm ×&#160;57.7&#160;mm (2.85&#160;in ×&#160;2.27&#160;in) and weighed a mean of 120.8&#160;g (4.26&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Peshkov_159-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peshkov-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Two eggs in Altai weighed 106 and 111&#160;g (3.7 and 3.9&#160;oz), respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The incubation stage lasts around 45 days, though may be up to week briefer in some cases.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Hatching is often sometime in May, but can continue to early June.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich2_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich2-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_young_and_parental_behaviour">Development of young and parental behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Development of young and parental behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg/220px-%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg/330px-%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg/440px-%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2384" data-file-height="1828" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%92%D0%9A%D0%9E_-_%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E2-1_%D0%BF%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86_%D0%B8_%D1%8F%D0%B9%D1%86%D0%BE_25-05-07.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A lone young nestling steppe eagle in a Russian nest.</div></div></div>
<p>The brood size in Kalmykia averaged 1.64.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> As for Transbaikal, the average number of chick per occupied nest was 0.65 while, in the successful nests, the average was 1.38.<sup id="cite_ref-Dauria_156-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dauria-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In the Altai, the mean brood size was reported as 2 in a sample of 9 one year and 1.4 in a sample of 10 the next year.<sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> A different study of the Altai found that there was a mean brood size of 1.86 per successful nest (0.86 per all occupied nests).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin5_207-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In the trans-border of Russia and Kazakhstan, the mean brood size per occupied nest was 1.03.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin6_199-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin6-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The average brood size in the Aral-Caspian was 2.36.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the highlands of the <a href="/wiki/East_Kazakhstan_Region" title="East Kazakhstan Region">East Kazakhstan Region</a>, a mean of 1.9 eaglets were found in 15 occupied nests.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> In the west Kazakh region, the brood size average was 2.22.<sup id="cite_ref-Coexistence_211-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coexistence-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> One study found that sex can be identified via morphometric measurements in 90% of cases and that the larger eastern populations of steppe eagle are conspicuously larger at all stages of development than the more westerly ones.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> The growth and development of a single chick in the <a href="/wiki/Zhanybek_District" title="Zhanybek District">Zhanybek District</a> of Kazakhstan was well studied, in an area where little ground squirrels were broadly available (i.e. about 40 adults per ha). This eaglet weighed 119&#160;g (4.2&#160;oz) and was covered in white down on day 1 while, by day 6, it weighed 331&#160;g (11.7&#160;oz) and had down white as earlier but longer. By day 10, it weighed 602&#160;g (1.327&#160;lb) and by day 15 weighed 1,060&#160;g (2.34&#160;lb). At the age of 20 days, this eaglet weighed 1.65&#160;kg (3.6&#160;lb) and manifest much more conspicuous emerging brown feathers. Once aged 25 days, it weighed 2.01&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb) and had juvenile feathers over a third of its body and by day 30 it weighed 2.3&#160;kg (5.1&#160;lb). By day 35, it weighed 2.7&#160;kg (6.0&#160;lb) and was almost all brown but with down still about the head. Full body size and juvenile plumage (but for fully-developed wing and tail feathers) was attained at 40–43 days for the chick, it weighed 2.9&#160;kg (6.4&#160;lb); although fully grown, it still crouched down at threats and could not fly.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Similar growth was tracked in Xinjiang, where it was noted that around 20 days of age, the young could standing, wing flap frequently at 45 days and move about the nest vicinity somewhat and could at 60 days old eat unaided.<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In one case near the Irtysh River, when a nest was approached by humans, the eldest eaglet was seen to aggressively display and try to displace them while it appeared to protect its two young siblings, which sheltered behind it.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The fledging of the young eagles occurs relatively quickly at somewhere between 55–65 days, due probably to the vulnerability of the nest sites, quickly leaving the nest is probably advantages to avoid dangers peculiar to these eagles nest like predators, <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>, cattle-trampling, humans and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Usually, the second fledgling initially flies somewhat later and more clumsily than the first.<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich2_146-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich2-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The difference in fledgling times from the first to the second fledgling was recorded to be 8 to 10 days in Transbaikal.<sup id="cite_ref-Peshkov_159-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peshkov-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The mother steppe eagle in Kalmykia is not as tight a sitter as other <i>Aquila</i> eagles tend to be and flushed when approached within 50 to 100&#160;m (160 to 330&#160;ft) and may too take a relatively long time to return. However, here the females were highly tolerant of automobiles.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Along the <a href="/wiki/Irtysh_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Irtysh River">Irtysh River</a> in Kazakhstan, although steppe eagles flushed when approached within 100&#160;m (330&#160;ft) they did not depart for long and returned quickly for this species relative to other reports.<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich3_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich3-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Those nesting in Atyrau on utility towers allowed approach to within 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft) via car.<sup id="cite_ref-Gissov_209-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gissov-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> In the southern Aktobe region, the steppe eagles appear almost desensitized to humans, probably due to extensive exposure unlike in more remote areas, and allowed approach via motorcycle to within 3 to 4&#160;m (9.8 to 13.1&#160;ft) but flushed if a person was on foot within 5&#160;m (16&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Boyko_208-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyko-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Levels of <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">hemoparasites</a> appear to be low in nestling steppe eagles but the sample sizes of the only study known so far are small.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting_success_rates">Nesting success rates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Nesting success rates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Out of 10 nests in two locations in Kalmykia, alarmingly, 7 contained dead embryos that never hatched. Thus, the breeding success rate here was quite low at 30–40%.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlovich_139-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlovich-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Study from the Altai region determined that than occupied nests produced an average of 1.52 fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Within the Ukok Plateau part of the Altais, 31.6% of 19 checked nests were found to be successful.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov2_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov2-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> The mean number of fledglings in Mongolia per nest was 0.9 with a fledgling success rate of 42.2%, with no strong annual variations.<sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Nest located on cliffs were more successful in Mongolia, the reasons inferred were greater protection from the elements and from predators, while for those nesting on artificial substrates, the success rate was lower (37.5%).<sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Of 30 failures recorded in Mongolia, 37.5% were due to desertion by the parents, 16.7% due to <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">infertile eggs</a>, 6.7% due to predation (possibly from wolves and <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>)), 10% due to starvation, 3.3% from <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">cannibalism</a> and the remaining 26.7% from unknown causes.<sup id="cite_ref-Gombobaatar_154-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombobaatar-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In the Lake Baskunchak area, several nests were abandoned due to reportedly accidental human intrusions.<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich2_146-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich2-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Study in Xinjiang indicated that about two-thirds of nesting attempts by steppe eagles there appear to fail.<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tuva" title="Tuva">Tuva</a>, the dictating factors of nesting success were considered habitat quality, food supply, disturbance levels, and the ability to rapidly change home ranges as habitats were unnaturally altered via the <a href="/wiki/Clearcutting" title="Clearcutting">felling</a> of tall trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Baranov_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baranov-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Starvation and dehydration seem to be leading causes of chick mortality in Lake Baskunchak.<sup id="cite_ref-Nikolaevich2_146-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikolaevich2-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In the Orenburg region, 41.1% of nestling mortality was due to starvation (after a decline of the little ground squirrel), 38.3% due to <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">steppe fires</a>, human disturbance at just under 10% and more minor causes were parental inexperience and predation.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin6_199-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin6-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Among active nests in the Karaganda Region, as of 2017, 42.26% were successful and a high rate of 54.46% completely failed, producing 0.61 fledglings per occupied nest and 1.45 per successful nest. Many Karaganda nests were noted to include infertile eggs, while many nests were destroyed in steppe fires.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin3_147-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin3-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> A follow-up study 2 years later found even more severe nest failure rates, with only 28.42% of nests succeeding. The reduction of the number of occupied nests here was 18.9% and by number of successful nests, the reduction was 63.9%.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin7_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin7-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> The breeding of a large quantity of visibly younger eagles in a breeding population is generally thought to indicate stress on the population of a raptor species.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> The steppe eagle, in sync with its overall decline, has shown an alarming increase of subadult specimens breeding. In Kalmykia, the number of breeding subadults increased from 1.75% prior to 5.26% during the 2011–2015 period.<sup id="cite_ref-Kalmykia_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kalmykia-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> This is a relatively small amount of breeding underaged eagles compared to some other populations. In the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian</a> areas, 39.62% of 58 breeding pairs contained 1 subadult breeding bird of around 3–5 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, within the Ukok Plateau, only 23.8% of 67 sighted steppe eagle were mature adults, indicating the reduction of mature individuals is similarly severe there.<sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov2_206-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov2-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Subadult breeding steppe eagles were also noticed in Mongolia.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steppe_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg/220px-Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg/330px-Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg/440px-Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1365" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Eagle_at_sunrise.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steppe eagle and the sunrise in <a href="/wiki/Tal_Chhapar_Sanctuary" title="Tal Chhapar Sanctuary">Tal Chhapar Sanctuary</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg/220px-Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg/330px-Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg/440px-Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1279" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis_Steppe_Eagle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Powerline electrocutions are depleting the population of steppe eagles in many parts of the range.</div></div></div>
<p>Densities of steppe eagles vary greatly both regionally and annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> This species has specialized food requirements, making this species more dependent on food availability than many other raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> European Russia in the 1990s was estimated to hold up to 20,000 pairs while steppe eagles were considered very rare in some parts of breeding range (i.e. central-south Siberia).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Even in recent decades, the steppe eagle has been considered easily one of the most numerous migrating eagles in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> The species has largely been regarded as "widespread and common" in winter in Indian subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_12-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande_171-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Estimates of the world population were projected based on the total breeding range encompasses over 8,000,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3,100,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) and average density very roughly of 50&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (19&#160;sq&#160;mi), which would put the population would be in six figures, but density were perceived to be slightly lower (i.e. 1 pair/100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi)) gives a total of 80,000 breeding pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> A refined and more recent estimate of the global population posited that there were 53,000–86,000 remaining breeding pairs globally for steppe eagles, with 43,000–59,000 pairs estimated in Kazakhstan, 2000–3000 estimated in Russia, 6000–13,000 estimated in Mongolia, and 2000–6000 estimated in China. This study projects the global number to be between 185,000 and 344,000 individuals at peak times, which is the end of the breeding season, with only 17,700–43,000 remaining adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Modern_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modern-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The Aral-Caspian area is estimated to hold about 5742–7548 breeding pairs (51% of which are thought to breed in <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, the remainder in Kazakhstan). Post-breeding, the Aral-Caspian is thought to hold about 10,000–14,000 individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is thought that the <a href="/wiki/West_Kazakhstan_Region" title="West Kazakhstan Region">West Kazakhstan Region</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalmykia" title="Kalmykia">Kalmykia</a> region are the epicenter of the world population, holding the maximum <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Haplotype" title="Haplotype">haplotype</a>) in the world, with the genetic diversity narrowing farther east in the breeding range.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sayan_Mountains" title="Sayan Mountains">Sayan</a> region is thought to hold 43–51% of the current breeding population of steppe eagles in Russia, with the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Republic" title="Altai Republic">Altai Republic</a> estimated to hold 270–280 breeding pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin4_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin4-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> A slight recovery has been noted in the <a href="/wiki/Tuva" title="Tuva">Tuva Basin</a>, going up to as much as 200–300 pairs between 2008 and 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-Baranov_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baranov-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg/220px-%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg/330px-%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg/440px-%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%9E25_%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%93%D0%BD_8_corr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steppe eagle mother with its eaglet; this eagle is still quite young and not in adult plumage. Subadult breeding is generally considered indicative of population stress in raptors.</div></div></div>
<p>The breeding range of the steppe eagle was already contracted markedly early on in 20th century, especially in the west, largely as result of habitat loss (in particular appropriation of steppes for agriculture) but also persecution and predation, factors that may have drove some pairs to elevated nest sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Steppe eagles once bred in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a> and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Careless <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> use in Europe depleted prey populations and collapsed nearly the whole local ecosystem, which alongside habitat conversions and persecution drove the steppe eagle's European breeding population to <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extinction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Range contraction is very considerable today in the <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a> area where it is almost locally extinct as well.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Declines overall have been rapid and alarming.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_10-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is estimated that as of the 20 years prior to 2015, the population worldwide has declined at minimum by 58.6%.<sup id="cite_ref-Increase_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Increase-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the steppe eagle was uplisted in 2015 to being <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Increase_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Increase-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> In the borderlands of Russia and Kazakhstan, an estimated 11.9% population decline was detected in merely 6 years of study.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin6_199-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin6-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Primary global threats appear to be <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persecution" title="Persecution">persecution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>, predation (and trampling by <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>) of chicks and <a href="/wiki/Electrocution" title="Electrocution">electrocutions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird_strike" title="Bird strike">wire collisions</a>, especially the latter causes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the steppe eagles genetic diversity may be rapidly declining as well.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> The diagnosed causes of decline in Xinjiang, Tibet and <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a> were found mostly to be <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poisoning" title="Poisoning">poisoning</a> from <a href="/wiki/Rodenticide" title="Rodenticide">rodent control programs</a> (with systemic efforts dating back at least 60 years), poisoning also targeted towards predators, <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_trade" title="Wildlife trade">illegal trade</a>, food shortages and wire collisions but perhaps most of all <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>, often with their former homes destroyed to make way for roadworks, tourism and mine exploration, with more destruction from overharvest of trees and plants and overgrazing by livestock, and accidental are frequent.<sup id="cite_ref-Maming_138-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maming-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Poisoning is thought to be quite prevalent in the Altais, as well as powerline electrocutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Markovich_187-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markovich-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> A mean of 13.3 individual steppe eagles in Kazakhstan were estimated to be killed by each 10&#160;km (6.2&#160;mi) of powerline.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin7_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin7-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> The steppe eagle can even been the most frequently electrocuted raptor in Kazakh data, at up to about 35% of 129 dead raptors or 49% of 223 dead raptors in a couple of relatively small stretches.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Many birds of various families are killed by these powerlines, as was recorded in Central Kazakhstan, in addition to the various raptorial birds which (due to their low reproductive rates and large territories) are often unable to withstand continuous powerline losses.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> It is estimated that in West Kazakhstan that no fewer than 1635 nests (or 7.9% of the entire breeding Kazakh population) fail due to the parents being electrocuted on powerlines.<sup id="cite_ref-Coexistence_211-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coexistence-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Across the border in <a href="/wiki/Orenburg" title="Orenburg">Orenburg</a>, Russia, a high rate of electrocutions is known be occurring as well.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, overgrazing and habitat alterations by humans have destroyed much of what remains around <i><a href="/wiki/Haloxylon" title="Haloxylon">Haloxylon</a></i> stands of west Kazakhstan (in turn destroying about 50% of local nesting attempts), while some steppe eagles cannot nest locally due to presumed competitive exclusion by imperial eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Coexistence_211-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coexistence-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Locally predation and nest losses can claim up to 80% of chicks but productivity is heavily dependent on foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Karaganda_Region" title="Karaganda Region">Karaganda Region</a>, 20.9% of nests were recorded to be ruined by steppe fires.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin7_222-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin7-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Less well understood losses in West Kazakhstan may be due to continued poaching, <a href="/wiki/Diclofenac" title="Diclofenac">poisonings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_fly" title="Black fly">blackflies</a> which kill nestlings and seem to have increased with the <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">warming temperatures</a>. The number of steppe fires appears to be higher than ever before which also may be due to increasing warmth and aridity.<sup id="cite_ref-Increase_231-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Increase-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Mateos_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Mateos-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin9_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin9-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The probable and irrevocable extinction of this species is projected if numerous detrimental factors are not reversed, namely the mitigation of electrical lines and towers in breeding areas, the removal of grazing and manmade fires in breeding areas as well as habitat destruction and the several other threats.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Mateos_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Mateos-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Ambiguities exist over whether Kazakhstan can institute protective laws strong enough to prevent the loss of the species, as even powerline alterations have not occurred at the national level. However, continued relative stability of the species has been detected in the more minor eastern part of the range (based on largely unchanged numbers of migrants from here in Nepal and elsewhere in the Himalayas) such as Altai.<sup id="cite_ref-Increase_231-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Increase-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> One potential stopgap solution to mitigate some of the electrocutions may be to install T-shaped perches around transmission towers which has been effective in reducing the more minor decline from powerlines in the Mongolian part of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Notably, the rate of decline in the western part of the range is so pronounced which is quite to the contrary other similar raptors like eastern imperial eagles and long-legged buzzards have began to recover in similar areas (the opposite pattern almost manifests in the imperial eagle, which is declining more several in the east such as Baikal).<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin9_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin9-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
